SECTION 1.  PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
The Honouliuli/Waipahu/Pearl City Wastewater Facilities Plan project involves a 
feasibility assessment and preliminary engineering for a deep, large diameter tunnel to 
store and convey wastewater generated by existing wastewater facilities in the 
Ewa District, to the Honouliuli Wastewater Treatment Plant, on the Island of Oahu, 
Hawaii. This geotechnical data report presents the results of our field exploration and 
laboratory testing performed for the preliminary evaluation and design of the tunnel 
storage/conveyance. The project location and general vicinity are shown on the 
Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
Based on the information provided, we understand that two design tunnel 
alignments with various shaft locations are considered in the project, as shown on the 
Overall Site Plans, Plates 2.1 through 2.3.  A summary of the field exploration program 
conducted for the project is presented below. Detailed information and discussions for 
each specific work item are provided in the respective appendices of this geotechnical 
data report.

Due to available subsurface information from the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit 
Corridor Project (HHCTCP), some of the borings initially planned along Farrington and 
Kamehameha Highways were relocated.  As a result, two off-shore borings were 
planned within the Middle Loch and East Loch.  However, due to the extended time to 
obtain Right-of-Entry from the Navy, these two off-shore borings were deleted to avoid 
delay to the project schedule. 
Based on the current authorization for releasing HHCTCP data (East Kapolei to 
Pearl Highlands Segment and Kamehameha Highway Segment), relevant borings from 
these segments within the tunnel alignment were selected, as shown on the Site Plans, 
Plates 3.2 through 3.7.  The boring logs for these selected borings are included in 
Appendix I (East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands Segment) and Appendix J 
(Kamehameha Highway Segment). 
A geophysical exploration was planned to help refine the profile lines between 
the soil and rock formation, especially in the areas over the water and geological soil 
and rock interface. However, the geophysical survey in the areas over the water was 
deleted due to difficulty in obtaining the ROE from the Navy.  The geophysical survey 
was only conducted along the existing bike path which is outside of the Navy’s property 
for a total of nine segments.  The results of the geophysical exploration are presented in 
the report, entitled “Report for the Geophysical Survey along the Honouliuli Sewer 
Tunnel Alignment, Oahu, Hawaii”, dated September 29, 2010, by Global Geophysics.  
The report is included in Appendix C.

SECTION 2.  SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program is for the 
preliminary evaluation and design of the tunnel storage and conveyance system for the 
Honouliuli/Waipahu/Pearl City Wastewater Facilities Plan project.  Our work was 
performed in general accordance with the scopes of services outlined in our fee 
proposal dated July 30, 2008 and the Work Order/Task Order No. 9 from AECOM, 
dated July 28, 2009. In order to accomplish this, we conducted a field exploration and 
laboratory testing program consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of available in-house geologic and soils information 
in the project vicinity.  
2. 
Preparation and submittal of a field exploration work plan for the 
subsurface exploration. 
3. 
Preparation and submittal of an accident prevention plan with activity 
hazard analysis and Best Management Practice (BMP) plan in support of 
our field exploration activities. 
4. 
Coordination with the State of Hawaii – Department of Transportation, 
Highways Division; the City and County of Honolulu – Department of 
Parks and Recreation; and the City and County of Honolulu - Department 
of Planning and Permitting to obtain the necessary excavation permits for 
the boring locations within the public right-of-way. 
5. 
Coordination with the City and County of Honolulu – Department of 
Environmental Services to obtain the Right-of-Entry permit to the existing 
wastewater treatment plant and wastewater pump stations prior to our 
field exploration work at the boring locations located within their property. 
6. 
Coordination with the Navy and Army Corps of Engineers to obtain the 
Right-of-Entry and Permits for entering the East Loch and Middle Loch for 
off-shore drilling. 
7. 
Coordination with private property owners for access through their 
property onto the tunnel alignment for the field exploration and 
geophysical surveying. 
8. 
Coordination of utility toning and clearance of the boring locations with 
various utility companies.

SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu is generally composed of the weathered and dissected 
remnants of two extinct shield (domed) volcanoes – Waianae and Koolau. The older 
Waianae Volcano forms the bulk of the western third of Oahu’s landmass while the 
younger Koolau Volcano forms a major portion of the eastern two-thirds of Oahu’s 
landmass. Generally, it is believed that the Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes 
began erupting on the order of about 20 to 25 million years ago as deep ocean 
submarine volcanoes. Subaerial volcanic eruptions on the Island of Oahu ended 
approximately 2.2 to 3.5 million years ago in the Pleistocene Epoch. Furthermore, the 
Waianae Volcano became extinct while the Koolau Volcano remained active; therefore 
the eastern flank of Waianae Volcano is partially overlapped and buried below the 
younger Koolau Volcano lavas near the central interior portions of the Island of Oahu. 
Following the cessation of the major Koolau volcanic activity, a long period of 
erosion was experienced before some late-stage volcanic activity resumed with the 
eruption of the Honolulu Volcanic Series approximately 400,000 to 800,000 years ago, 
mainly at the southern and southeastern portions of the Island of Oahu. The late stage 
eruption of the Honolulu Volcanic Series is responsible for some additional localized 
lava flows and the formation of several prominent pyroclastic cinder and tuff cones in 
the Honolulu area and vicinity. 
In addition to the primary volcanic composition of the Island of Oahu, other 
secondary and generally more localized geologic earth materials comprise the lowland, 
coastal, and valley portions of the island. The secondary earth materials include various 
alluvial and colluvial deposits, oceanic and marine sediments, and submerged and 
emerged coralline reef materials. 
The proposed sewage tunnel alignments traverse along the lower distal flanks of 
the Waianae and Koolau Mountain Ranges (Ewa Plain) and extend across lowland 
coastal alluvial plains (East Loch). The Ewa Plain is a gently sloping land surface 
formed by the long-term accumulation of alluvial sediments consisting mainly of 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii ● California 
terrigenous clays and silts with some embedded cobbles and boulders derived from the 
weathering and decomposition of basaltic rock residing further upslope. Some pockets 
of marine sediments and coralline detritus materials were intercalated within the alluvial 
sediments as a result of past sea-level fluctuations. 
The Ewa Plain is dissected by various drainage gullies, which emanate in the 
upland areas and discharge into the Middle and East Lochs of Pearl Harbor. With the 
passage of time, some of the incised drainage channels meandered in course and were 
filled-in by more recent soft alluvial sediments. The deeper basaltic rock of the 
Waianae and Koolau Mountain Ranges is the primary aquifer and potable water source 
on the Island of Oahu. The subsurface geological conditions along the sewage tunnel 
alignments reflect past glacio-eustatic sea-level fluctuations, which occurred most 
substantially during the Pleistocene Epoch. 
The East Loch of Pearl Harbor resides at the confluence of several 
well-established natural drainage channels emanating from the higher elevations of the 
Koolau Mountain Range. The drainages extend in a generally north to south orientation 
across the sewage tunnel alignment. The meandering streams have contributed 
significantly to the erosional evolution and deposition of the coastal alluvial plains by 
incising into the underlying basaltic rock (Koolau Basalt) comprising the basement rock 
formation of this portion of the Island of Oahu. 
As mentioned previously, the subsurface geological conditions along the sewage 
tunnel alignments are typified by various terrigenous alluvial deposits with some 
embedded marine sediments (coralline detritus) overlying buried volcanic materials 
consisting of residual and saprolitic soils and weathered basaltic rock formation. The 
base of the stratigraphic section in this area is the volcanic materials associated with the 
Waianae and Koolau Basalt.  
The sedimentary wedge overlying the buried basaltic rock acts as a confining low 
permeable layer overlying the more permeable basal aquifer consisting of basaltic rock 
materials. On the Ewa Plain and vicinity, the sedimentary wedge of alluvial materials is 
also referred to as the “caprock” overlying the basal aquifer. The “caprock” keeps the 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii ● California 
basal aquifer under a confining water pressure; therefore, some artesian flow conditions 
may be encountered at the ground surface when the basal aquifer with sufficient head is 
pierced and a conduit is provided extending toward the ground surface. 
In general, groundwater levels in the Southern Oahu regional aquifer area may 
range from about 25 to 30 feet above sea level inland to about 15 to 20 feet above sea 
level near the shore where the groundwater is under artesian pressure beneath the 
confining caprock material (USGS Publication HA 730-N). 
The following paragraphs provide a generalized description of the major 
processes that contributed to the geomorphic evolution of the condition in the immediate 
vicinity of the proposed sewage tunnel. 
The Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes formed during the late Pliocene and 
early Pleistocene Epochs by the sequential deposition of basaltic lava flows. As 
mentioned previously, the main shield-building activity ceased about 2.2 to 3.5 million 
years ago. Evidence from previous deep drilled wells indicates that the Island of 
Oahu has subsided by as much as 1,200 feet since the cessation of this early volcanic 
activity (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During that period of slow island subsidence, coral-algal reefs grew and 
fossilized along the southern coast of the Island of Oahu. As a result, broad 
embayments formed that were bounded by fringing barrier reefs whose growth 
essentially kept pace with the slow rate of island subsidence. Thereafter, a series of 
shallow lagoons formed behind the barrier reefs and both terrigenous and marine 
sediments accumulated within the lagoons (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (most recent Ice Age), many sea-level fluctuations 
occurred as a result of the waxing and waning of global continental glaciation. Generally 
speaking, as the great continental glaciers accumulated, the global level of the oceans 
fell because less water was available to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the 
glaciers receded (melted), the global level of the oceans rose because of the water 
influx. The landmass elevation of the Island of Oahu remained essentially stable during 
these sea-level changes; therefore, the fluctuations were eustatic in nature. The 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii ● California 
glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of the sea that were either higher or lower 
relative to the present sea-level conditions. The following are some generalized 
examples of the geomorphic effects that resulted from the changes in sea level relative 
to the island landmass. 
The higher sea-level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and depositional 
fans composed of terrigenous sediment at the heads of old bays. Furthermore, the 
higher sea stands caused accumulation of coralline reef materials at the 
correspondingly higher elevations and the deposition of lagoonal and marine sediments 
in quiet backwaters protected from wave action by fringing coral reefs. 
The lower sea-level stands caused streams to incise erosional valleys into the 
existing sediments and reef platforms. In addition, the subaerial exposure of the marine 
sediments and calcareous materials caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials 
and/or lagoonal deposits and hardening of the calcareous reef materials. Finally, 
renewed subaerial erosion (acting on the upper regions of the volcanic dome) caused 
the mobilization and deposition of terrigenous alluvial soils within relatively high energy 
depositional environments, which is typified by a poorly sorted mix of alluvial materials 
with a greater range in particle sizes. 
During periods of no significant sea level change, continued stream flow 
extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and allowed the deposition of alluvium 
overlying pre-existing lagoonal sediments. 
It should be noted that two existing pyroclastic tuff cone vents (Aliamanu and 
Makalapa Craters) related to the late Honolulu Volcanic Series eruptions are present 
approximately 1 mile toward the southeast from the eastern end of the sewage tunnel 
alignments near the Aloha Stadium. Therefore, the eastern end of the proposed tunnel 
alignments may be overlain by Honolulu Volcanics (e.g., the Tuff). 
Land development within the last 100 years along the proposed sewage tunnel 
alignments has brought the area to its present form. Agricultural land use and other 
development projects have resulted in alterations to the natural terrain including the 
placement of earth fills.

3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In 
general, 
we 
observed 
six 
main 
stratigraphic 
units 
within 
the 
Honouliuli/Waipahu/Pearl City Wastewater Facilities Plan project. The stratigraphic units 
consist of soil and rock materials of varying character and engineering properties. The 
six main stratigraphic units are listed below in the general order of geologic age, 
beginning with the oldest unit and ending with the youngest unit: 
Koolau Basalt (Tkb) 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
Fill (Rf) 
The following sections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of the 
above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, past experience with similar 
materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at 
selected locations along the project alignment. The approximate delineation of these 
stratigraphic units at the ground surface along the alignment is presented on the 
Geology Map, Plate 4. 
3.2.1 Koolau Basalt (Tkb) 
Koolau Basalt is the oldest geologic unit within the sewage tunnel alignments 
extending from Honouliuli to Pearl City/Aiea. Generally, it consists of pahoehoe 
flows of basaltic lavas and may have a mantle of cobbles and boulders, or residual 
soils, overlying the top of the basalt rock formation. In addition, it is anticipated that 
various types of volcanic rock sub-components, such as clinker layers and lava 
tubes, also may be encountered within the basalt rock formation. 
Structurally, Koolau Basalt and its subunits are among the strongest of all the units 
encountered within the project alignment under consideration. Therefore, the basalt 
formation and its related residual soils and saprolites have high strength 
characteristics and moderate to low permeability in an undisturbed state. The 
following subunits of the Koolau Basalt are used in this geotechnical data report: 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii ● California 
Residual Soil 
This subunit consists of soils produced from the in-situ chemical 
weathering of the basic rock originating from Koolau Basalt. These soils 
generally consist of stiff to very stiff, brownish red or reddish brown clayey 
silts and silty clays. The soils are usually “structureless” and may have a 
blocky texture. 
Saprolite 
Saprolite is essentially derived from the tropical weathering of basaltic 
formations, which may contain intrusive rock bodies of variable 
composition. Saprolite is a form of residual soil which exhibits a relict 
structure from the parent rock. Structural features, such as vesicles and 
joints, are visible in saprolite. This subunit consists predominantly of 
clayey silt and silty clay soils containing variations in color with varying 
degrees of mottling. 
Clinker 
Clinker is the fragmental basalt found on the top, and sometimes at the 
bottom, of a’a lava flows. Clinker generally is gravel to cobble-sized 
fragments of scoriaceous rock in a tightly packed but open-graded 
structure. 
Basalt Formation 
Basalt formation is used to designate the in-situ lava formation. This 
subunit occurs as red, brown, or gray, severely fractured to massive, 
medium hard to very hard rock formation. 
3.2.2 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time 
by more recent deposits to become somewhat consolidated, and then subsequently 
uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit 
generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments, which 
have been transported by stream action from the upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channel are sometimes encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian 
groundwater from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii ● California 
may resemble saprolite soil deposits but are clearly differentiable by the layered 
characteristics. 
Older Alluvium materials generally exhibit low permeability (with exception of the 
coarse-grained basaltic cobbles and boulders) and high strength with generally low 
settlement characteristics. Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high 
shrink/swell potential when subjected to fluctuations in moisture content. 
3.2.3 Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital 
debris deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of 
any coralline rock assemblage. The detrital deposits are weakly to strongly 
cemented coralline debris consisting of gravel, sand, and silt sized clastic 
fragments. Some of the coralline fragments were dissolved and the solution cavities 
filled with cemented and/or uncemented fine sands and silts. 
Due to the granular nature of the majority of the coral-algal deposits and the 
variable degree of cementation frequently present, this formation generally has high 
to moderate in-situ permeability and strength characteristics. The following subunits 
are used in this geotechnical data report: 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The 
soils generally consist of medium dense to dense sandy gravel and 
gravelly sands. Silts and clays are only present in minor or trace amounts. 
Detritus also may be weakly cemented with occasional moderate to strong 
cementation. 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original 
growth positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to 
well cemented. 
3.2.4 Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
Tuff from vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series consists of volcanic ash, which 
consolidated or hardened to form a medium hard rock that resembles siltstone or 
sandstone, locally called “mudrock.” The tuff is frequently found overlying 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii ● California 
coralline deposits or alluvium units. It should be noted that it is quite common to 
see a coarsening from accidental detritus and/or agglomeration toward the 
bottoms of the ash beds.  
Because the formation is a medium hard rock, it has very high strength 
characteristics. Structurally, the tuff is very competent. This formation has 
relatively low permeability, and groundwater generally is not encountered in this 
formation. When it does occur, it is usually in the form of seepage along joints. 
3.2.5 Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils are generally the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
accumulated very rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated sediments, which 
settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. Recent alluvium 
is generally found within some stream channels and bordering some stream banks, 
at the mouths (outlets) of streams, and within broad valley floors and basin features 
surrounded by hills. These soils also may be found in proximity to swamp and tidal 
flat environments. 
The soils assigned to this unit generally are considered to have low in-situ 
permeability and have low strength characteristics and the tendency for excessive 
settlement to occur in structures overlying soils of this unit. The following subunits 
are used in this geotechnical data report. 
Recent Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii ● California 
Estuarine/Marsh Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with 
fibrous organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit 
contains traces of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized 
by a moderate to strong organic odor and may contain small fragile 
mollusk shells. 
3.2.6 Fill (Rf) 
This stratigraphic unit represents low lying areas which were filled during 
urbanization. The majority of these low lying areas is underlain at relatively shallow 
depths by recent alluvium and was originally marsh areas. Some of the filled lands 
along the sewage tunnel alignment are underlain by older alluvial soil deposits. In 
areas underlain by the recent alluvium, the fill materials generally consist of clayey 
silts and sandy silts with stiff to hard consistencies. The fills only form a thin crust 
over the soft alluvial soils. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were 
frequently placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to make 
any general statement regarding their engineering properties or construction in 
these areas. 
Groundwater in the fill areas generally is expected to occur as a basal water table 
near sea level. However, higher perched groundwater tables may be encountered. 
3.3 
Groundwater 
We encountered groundwater in the borings drilled along the project alignments 
at depths ranging from about 3 to 42.9 feet below the existing ground surface at the 
time of our field exploration. The ground elevations at each boring location were 
estimated based on Google Earth and topographic maps from previous projects in the 
vicinity. Based on these estimated ground elevations, the groundwater levels measured 
generally correspond to about Elevations -4 to +15.4 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL). It 
should be noted that groundwater levels could fluctuate depending on seasonal 
precipitation, groundwater withdrawal and/or injection, and other factors. 
SECTION 3. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii ● California 
Although artesian groundwater was not encountered in the borings drilled for this 
study, it was encountered in the borings drilled for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit 
Corridor Project in the Waipahu and Pearl City areas.  
It should be noted that portions of the tunnel alignment are located within the 
Pearl Harbor artesian aquifer basins. As discussed previously, artesian groundwater 
occurs within the Koolau Basalt. The potentiometric head of this aquifer varies spatially, 
temporarily and in response to pumpage. Therefore, artesian groundwater should be 
anticipated at the location where the tunnel alignments encounter Koolau Basalt at 
about Elevation +20 feet MSL or lower. 
END OF SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii ● California 
SECTION 4.  GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
The geotechnical exploration program conducted for the proposed sewage tunnel 
alignments generally consisted of the following field and laboratory work components. A 
brief description of each work component is presented below. Detailed discussions, 
findings, and test results of the various components are provided in the respective 
appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
Soil and rock sampling methods and field testing are described in more detail in 
the introduction to Appendix A. The following discussion provides an overview of the 
methods and procedures. 
Typically, the soil borings located on land were initially advanced with 4-inch 
diameter continuous-flight solid-stem augers and continued with PQ-size wireline coring 
methods to advance the PQ size casing. Sampling of the soils was performed using the 
modified California sampler (ASTM D 3550) and the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) 
split spoon sampler (ASTM D 1586). In the softer soils, relatively undisturbed samples 
were obtained by pushing a 3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel thin walled tube into 
the soil formation (ASTM D 1587).  
It should be noted that two off-shore borings were planned in the Middle Loch 
and East Loch. Due to difficulties obtaining ROE permission, two off-shore soil borings 
were deleted to avoid project delay.  
Where the borehole was advanced utilizing the core barrel (PQ or HQ) in the soil 
formations, samples of the soils recovered from the core barrel also were obtained and 
retained in core boxes for visual observation. In some cases, the borehole was 
advanced utilizing the PQ or HQ casing only as a rotary wash drilling method; therefore, 
there were no soil samples retrieved between the soil sampling points (soil sampling by 
the modified California sampler or the split spoon sampler for the Standard Penetration 
Test). The borings were advanced into the rock formations with the PQ-size or HQ-size 
wireline coring methods. The PQ-size and HQ-size wireline coring methods produce 
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii ● California 
rock core samples that are 3.35 inches (or 85 mm) and 2.5 inches (or 63.5 mm) in 
diameter, respectively. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which 
was performed in accordance with ASTM D 1586. SPT sampling provides a measure of 
the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, which 
is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration 
resistance of the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the 
modified California sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured 
as described in Appendix A. ASTM D 6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining 
Rock Quality Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ 
with core diameters of 36.5 mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally 
acceptable for measuring RQD as long as proper drilling techniques are used that do 
not cause excessive core breakage or poor recovery, or both. The NX-size (54.7 mm 
[2.16 in.]) and NQ-size (47.5 mm [1.87 in.]) are the optimal core sizes for measuring 
RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters provided the core diameter is 
clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in Appendix H for information 
and reference. 
During the field exploration program, our geologists performed pocket 
penetrometer and torvane tests on selected cohesive soil samples. Pocket 
penetrometer tests provide an indication of the unconfined compressive strength of a 
soil sample. Torvane tests provide an indication of the undrained shear strength of a soil 
sample. The pocket penetrometer and torvane testing values are shown on the Logs of 
Borings in Appendix A. 
In addition, the soil samples retrieved in the borings from the ground surface to 
the groundwater table, or 20 feet below the ground surface, whichever is less, were 
scanned using a Photo Ionizing Detector (PID).  PID readings provide an indication of 
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii ● California 
the potential contamination.  The PID readings are shown on the Logs of Borings in 
Appendix A. 
4.2 
Laboratory Testing 
Laboratory testing was performed on soil and rock samples to determine the 
physical and engineering properties. Laboratory tests included moisture content, unit 
weight, Atterberg limits, grain size distribution, unconsolidated-undrained (UU) triaxial 
compression, direct shear, rock unconfined compression, and corrosion potential tests 
(organic content, resistivity, pH, chloride content, and sulfate content). The laboratory 
test results are provided in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Geophysical Explorations 
Geophysical exploration was performed to delineate the approximate profile lines 
between soil and rock along the tunnel alignments.  The profile lines were initially 
planned in areas over water or lacking of subsurface boring information. Due to 
unexpectedly long duration for ROE permission, the profile lines in the area over water 
were deleted to avoid project delay.  Locations of the geophysical survey segments are 
summarized in the following table.  The results of the geophysical exploration are 
presented in the Appendix C.

4.4 
In-Situ Permeability Testing 
Packer injection tests were performed in selected coral and basalt formation 
intervals encountered in the borings.  The packer tests provide in-situ transmissivity 
(permeability) of the coral and basalt formation, to evaluate tunnel and shaft 
groundwater control requirements.  The test results are present in Appendix D. 
In addition, in-situ percolation tests using constant head or falling head methods 
with well point-filter were conducted in selected highly weathered rock formation with 
fine grain content or sandy/silty soils.  The percolation tests provide approximate in-situ 
coefficient of permeability.  The test results are present in Appendix D. 
4.5 
Piezometric Water Level Monitoring 
As previously mentioned, eight (8) borings were converted to groundwater 
monitoring wells using 2-inch I.D. Schedule 40 PVC pipe with 10 feet of slotted pipe 
(screen) installed at the bottom portion of the well. One vibrating wire piezometer was 
installed in each well, to monitor water levels and their seasonal fluctuations. Data was 
recorded with a datalogger from the time of installation (varying from January to 
March 2010) for a six-month monitoring period. Details of the piezometer installation 
and piezometer readings are included in Appendix E. 
It should be noted that unexpected higher ground water level was observed in 
the monitoring wells (Nos. 7 and 10). The data acquisition systems installed in the wells 
were submerged causing loss of data. In addition, unexpected groundwater level 
fluctuation was observed in the monitoring wells (Nos. 11 and 14). The groundwater 
level fluctuation may be related to seasonal precipitation, groundwater withdrawal 
and/or injection, and possible artesian aquifer basins in the vicinity.  
SECTION 4. GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 21 
Hawaii ● California 
4.6 
Rock Index Property Testing 
Brazilian tensile test, Cerchar abrasion index and thin petrographic analysis on 
selected coral and basalt rock samples were conducted by Colorado School of Mines. 
The test results are summarized in Tables F-1.1 through F-1.3 and presented in 
Appendix F. 
4.7 
Environmental Testing 
Environmental tests, including Volatile Organics, Semi-Volatile Organics, TPH, 
RCRA 8 Metals, and PCBs, were performed on selected soil samples for analytical 
constituents. The test results were sent directly from the environmental testing 
laboratory to AECOM. The test results are also included in Appendix G. 
4.8 
Additional Subsurface Information 
Selected boring logs from Geotechnical Data Report, Honolulu High-Capacity 
Transit Corridor Project, East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands, Ewa, Oahu, Hawaii dated 
March 27, 2009, and Geotechnical Data Report, Honolulu High-Capacity Transit 
Corridor Project, Kamehameha Highway Segment, Pearl City to Aiea, Ewa, Oahu, 
Hawaii dated February 16, 2010 are included in Appendices I and J, respectively, for 
information and reference purposes. These selected boring locations are presented on 
Plates 2.1 through 2.3 – Overall Site Plans and Plates 3.1 through 3.13 – Site Plans.

SECTION 5.  LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this geotechnical 
data report are based upon information obtained from the field exploration. Variations of 
subsurface conditions between and beyond the exploration points may occur, and the 
nature and extent of these variations may not become evident until additional field 
investigation and/or construction is underway. 
The locations and elevations of the field exploration points indicated herein have 
been estimated and measured from the ground features shown on Google Earth maps.  
The physical locations and elevations of the field exploration points should be 
considered accurate only to the degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field 
exploration points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, 
as such, may denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the logs of 
borings herein were based on measurements recorded in the borings at the times 
shown on the graphic representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water 
level may occur due to variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of AECOM and their client, 
City and County of Honolulu, Department of Environmental Services, for specific 
application to the Honouliuli/Waipahu/Pearl City Wastewater Facilities Plan project in 
accordance with generally accepted geotechnical engineering principles and practices.  
No warranty is expressed or implied. 
This geotechnical data report presents information on the general subsurface 
conditions for the preliminary evaluation and design of the proposed tunnel 
storage/conveyance system. This geotechnical data report is limited in scope and is for 
preliminary cost estimation purposes only. Therefore, this report may not contain 
sufficient data, or the proper information, to serve as a basis for final design and 
construction cost estimates. A contractor wishing to bid on this project is urged to retain 
SECTION 5. LIMITATIONS  
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 23 
Hawaii ● California 
a competent geotechnical engineer to assist in the interpretation of this report and/or in 
the performance of additional site-specific exploration for bid estimating purposes. 
This geotechnical data report was not intended to investigate the potential 
presence of hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should be noted that the 
equipment, techniques, and personnel used to conduct a geo-environmental exploration 
differ substantially from those applied in geotechnical engineering. 
END OF LIMITATIONS

APPENDIX A 
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
SEPTEMBER 2010     Page A-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   A 
Field Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions along the proposed sewage tunnel 
alignments by drilling and sampling 14 borings, designated as Boring Nos. B-1 through 
B-7, B-9 though B-14 and B-16. The borings extended to depths ranging from about 
92.5 to 186 feet below the existing ground surface using various truck-mounted drill rigs 
equipped with continuous-flight augers and rotary coring tools. The boring locations are 
shown on the Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.13. 
Soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch ID continuous-flight 
solid-stem augers and PQ/HQ-size wireline coring method to advance PQ/HQ size casing.  
After the borings were advanced to the maximum depth of the boreholes, the bored holes 
(with the exception of those being converted to groundwater monitoring wells) were 
generally backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite pellets (below the 
groundwater level) and bentonite chips (above the groundwater level). The temporary 
casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the bentonite pellets 
starting from the bottom and advancing upward.  No additives were added to the drilling 
water during our drilling operations. The top 3 feet of the borehole was filled with concrete. 
Our geologists classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D 2488, Standard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on 
a near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by 
testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D 2487, 
Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil 
Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered and other observations, such as 
groundwater levels, are presented on the Logs of Borings, Plates A-1.1 through A-16.5. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the borings drilled in 
general accordance with ASTM D 3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, 
Spilt Barrel, Drive Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler 
with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from 
the borings drilled in general accordance with ASTM D 1586, Standard Test Method for 
Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths.  
Relatively “undisturbed” samples of the soft soil formation were obtained by pushing a 
3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil formation in general 
accordance with ASTM D 1587, Standard Practice for Thin-Walled Tube Sampling of Soils 
for Geotechnical Purposes. 
Pocket penetrometer and torvane tests were performed on selected cohesive soil 
samples retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer and torvane tests provide 
Appendix A 
Field Exploration 
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
SEPTEMBER 2010     Page A-2 
Hawaii ● California 
indications of the unconfined compressive strength and undrained shear strength of the 
soil sample, respectively. Results of the pocket penetrometer and torvane tests are 
summarized on Tables B-1.1 through B-1.15 and are presented on the Logs of Borings at 
the appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ/HQ wireline coring system 
with core barrel (core size of 3.35 or 2.5 inches), in general accordance with 
ASTM D 2113, Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation.  Core drilling is a rotary drilling 
method that uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the 
hollow core of the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. Rock 
cores were described in general accordance with the Rock Description System, as shown 
on the Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.2.  The Rock Description System is based on the 
publication “Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock 
Masses” by International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977).  
Recovery (REC) may be used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the 
relative quality of rock masses, where appropriate. Recovery is defined as the actual 
length of material recovered from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. 
For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery 
would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run that is sound 
material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any drilling, 
mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is 
recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on 
the Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to describe the 
relative quality of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of 
Rocks and Minerals” by Robert S. Carmichael (1989).

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite

APPENDIX B 
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
SEPTEMBER 2010     Page B-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the project 
were visually examined and classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii.  Selected 
samples were tested to evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties 
of the soils encountered. The laboratory testing program was formulated with the 
emphasis on evaluating the moisture, density, plasticity, gradation, compressibility, 
shear strength, and corrosivity of the soils encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of dry density and natural 
moisture content, Atterberg Limits, sieve analysis, hydrometer, triaxial compression, 
direct shear, unconfined compression, and corrosion.  Results of the tests performed 
are summarized in Tables B-1.1 through B-3 and are presented on Plates B-1.1 through 
B-46. Detailed descriptions of the various tests performed for this exploration are 
presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture Content and Dry Density tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to aid in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and 
correlating the layers between test borings.  The moisture content tests were performed 
in general accordance with ASTM D 2216.  The test results are summarized in 
Tables B-1.1 through B-1.15 and are presented on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
Sixty-three (63) Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to evaluate the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in 
classifying the soils.  The Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance 
with ASTM D 4318.  The test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths and in Tables B-1.1 through B-1.15.  Graphic presentation of 
the test results are provided on Plates B-1 through B-7. 
Sieve Analysis and Hydrometer 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
fifty-nine (59) Sieve Analysis tests including thirty-five (35) Hydrometer tests were 
performed to evaluate the gradation characteristics and to aid in soil classification. The 
tests were performed in accordance with ASTM C 117 and C 136.  Graphic presentation 
of the test results are provided on Plates B-8 through B-19.  The test results are also 
summarized in Tables B-1.1 through B-1.15. 
Appendix B 
Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
SEPTEMBER 2010     Page B-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Unconsolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, twenty-two (22) 
unconsolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests were performed on selected 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 2850.  The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the “undisturbed” 
samples.  The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates B-20 
through B-41 and summarized in Tables B-2.1 through B-2.3. 
Direct Shear 
To 
evaluate 
the 
shear 
strength 
characteristics 
of 
the 
in-situ 
soils, 
two strained-controlled, consolidated-drained Direct Shear tests were performed on the 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 3080.  The test results are presented 
on Plates B-42 and B-43 and summarized in Tables B-2.1 through 2.3. 
Unconfined Compression – Rock Cores 
Seventy-nine (79) Unconfined Compression tests (ASTM D 7012 Method C) 
were performed on selected rock cores to evaluate their unconfined compressive 
strength of the rock formation encountered.  Unconfined compression test results are 
presented on Plates B-44 through B-46. 
Corrosion Test 
Twenty-two (22) sets of organic content (ASTM D 2974), pH (ASTM G 51) and/or 
minimum resistivity (ASTM G 57) tests were performed on selected soil samples 
obtained from our field exploration by our office. In addition, twenty (20) sets of chloride 
(EPA 300) and sulfate (EPA 300) were performed by TestAmerica, Inc. on selected soil 
samples obtained from our field exploration.  The test results are presented 
in Table B-3.

A P P E N D I X   C 
Geophysical Exploration 
Geophysical exploration was performed by Global Geophysics.  A total of 
nine segment profiles was conducted along the proposed Alignment A, where the soil 
boring information was not available.  The results of the geophysical exploration are 
presented in the report, entitled “Report for the Geophysical Survey along the 
Honouliuli Sewer Tunnel Alignment, Oahu, Hawaii”, dated September 29, 2010. 
Global Geophysics 
16651 White Mountain Road SE 
Monroe, WA  98272 
Tel: 425-890-4321 
Fax: 360-805-0259 
Global Geophysics 
September 29, 2010 
Our ref: 093-0809.000 
Geolabs, Inc. 
2006 Kalihi Street 
Honolulu, HI 96819 
Attention:  Mr. John Chen 
RE: 
REPORT FOR THE GEOPHYSICAL SURVEY ALONG THE 
HONOULIULI SEWER TUNNEL ALIGNMENT, OAHU, HAWAII 
This letter report presents the results of the geophysical surveys performed by Global 
Geophysics. The geophysical surveys were carried out in nine segments along the 
proposed Honouliuli Sewer Tunnel from September 8th to 17th, 2010 in Waipahu and 
Pearl City, Hawaii.  The objective of the investigation is to determine the depth to 
bedrock. 
GEOPHYSICAL METHODS AND FIELD PROCEDURES 
Multi-channel analysis of surface wave (MASW) and electrical resistivity imaging were 
used for this project. The following paragraphs describe the methods and field procedures. 
Multi-channel Analysis of Surface Wave (MASW) 
The Multichannel Analysis of Surface Waves (MASW) method determines variations in 
surface wave velocities with increasing distances and wavelengths.  The data from these 
measurements are used to model the shear wave velocities of the subsurface.  This 
information can then be used to infer rock/soil types, stratigraphy and soil conditions.   
The MASW survey requires a seismic source, to generate surface-waves, and at least 24 
geophones, to measure the ground response at increasing distances from the source.  
Surface waves are a special type of seismic wave whose propagation is confined to the 
near surface medium.  The depth of subsurface penetration of a surface-wave is directly 
proportional to its wavelength.  In a non-homogeneous medium, surface-waves are 
dispersive, i.e. each wavelength has a characteristic velocity stemming from subsurface 
heterogeneities.  The relationship between surface-wave velocity and wavelength is used 
to calculate the shear-wave velocity of the medium with increasing depth. 
Mr. John Chen 
September 29, 2010 
Honouliuli Sewer Tunnel Project 
2 
093-0809.000 
Global Geophysics 
The seismic source can be either active or passive, depending on the application and 
location of the survey.  Examples of active sources include explosives, weight-drops, and 
vibrating pads.  Examples of passive sources are drill rigs, road traffic, micro-tremors, 
and water-wave action (in near-shore environments). Geophone measures the arrival time 
of the various components of the surface wave-train traveling from the seismic source.  
The surface-wave velocity with respect to frequency (called the ‘dispersion curve’) is 
determined by measuring the delay time in wave propagation between the geophones. 
The dispersion curve is then matched to a theoretical dispersion curve using an iterative 
forward-modeling procedure.  The result is a profile of shear-wave velocity versus depth.  
This shear wave profile can be with used other parameters such as density, to estimate the 
dynamic shear modulus of the medium as a function of depth. 
The MASW survey was conducted using a Geometrics Geode 24-channel digital 
seismograph.  The sensors were Mark Products hydrophones placed at 20 ft spacing and 
the seismic energy source was a hopto excavator. 
The survey procedures include laying out the landstreamer and marking the stations every 
40 ft with spray paint along the alignment. After laying out the landstreamer, the 
excavator was used to pound on the ground to generate vibrations. The signal was 
detected by the geophones and recorded in a computer for further processing. Initial test 
was conducted to determine the optimal offset from the source to the first geophone. The 
optimal offset was 120 ft for this site.  
Electrical Resistivity Imaging (ERI) 
The electrical resistivity imaging technique maps differences in the electrical properties 
of geologic materials.  These differences can result from variations in lithology, water 
content, and pore-water chemistry.  The method involves transmitting an electric current 
into the ground between two electrodes and measuring the voltage between two other 
electrodes.  The direct measurement is an apparent resistivity of the area beneath the 
electrodes that includes deeper layers as the electrode spacing is increased.  Recent 
advances in technology permit rapid collection of multiple soundings, using up to 50 
electrodes for each spread.  The data are modeled to create a 2-D geo-electric cross-
section that is useful for mapping both vertical and horizontal variations of the subsurface 
strata. 
The data were acquired with an AGI SuperSting R8 using up to 56 electrodes spaced at a 
20 feet interval.  Once the electrode array was installed in the ground, multiple soundings 
were automatically carried out by the control unit.  Downloading and routine modeling of 
the data was done on-site to provide preliminary analysis and QA/QC of the data. These 
results were displayed on a color monitor as cross-section that highlight changes in 
resistivity with depth along the transect. 
Mr. John Chen 
September 29, 2010 
Honouliuli Sewer Tunnel Project 
3 
093-0809.000 
Global Geophysics 
ANALYSIS AND RESULTS 
The MASW survey was completed along Segments 1 through 4 and 6 through 9, and ERI 
survey was completed along Segment 5.  GPS readings were taken every 200 ft along the 
nine (9) segments with a handheld Garmin GPS unit. The accuracy of the positioning 
from this unit may be 10-20 ft. The locations of these segments are shown in Figures 1A, 
1B and 1C. 
After assessing the s-wave results from software packages developed by Kansas 
Geological Survey (KGS) and Oyo Geometrics, we chose the package developed by Oyo 
Geometrics to complete the data processing for this project. Due to large velocity contrast 
between the overburden and underlying basalt, the dispersion curve is almost vertical at 
the low frequency end at where the bedrock is present. That means the phase velocity 
picking for bedrock is not accurate. Based on the boring logs from boreholes near the 
segments, we use the contour line of s-wave velocity of 1400 ft/s – 1800 ft/s as the top of 
bedrock. 
The S-wave and resistivity profiles are presented in Figures 2 through 5.  
1. Segment 1 (west of Waipahu Depot Street, Figure 2):  
• The velocity contour line of 1500 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock varies between 30 and 102 ft below 
ground surface. 
2. Segment 2 (east of Waipahu Depot Street to Waipio Point Access Road, Figure 2):  
• The velocity contour line of 1800 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock is deeper than 150 ft from station 0 to 
station 2100, and decreases to 86 ft near east end of this segment.   
3. Segment 3 (west of Waiawa Stream, Figure 3):  
• The velocity contour line of 1400 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock is deeper than 150 ft from station 0 to 
station 500, and varies between approximately 50 ft and 150 ft in the east 
part of the segment.   
4. Segment 4 (east of Waiawa Stream to Lehua Avenue, Figure 3):  
• The velocity contour line of 1800 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock is deeper than 150 ft from station 0 to 
station 860, and increases from 150 ft at station 860 ft to 63 ft near station 
1380 ft.  
Mr. John Chen 
September 29, 2010 
Honouliuli Sewer Tunnel Project 
4 
093-0809.000 
Global Geophysics 
5. Segment 5 (Takano Nursery, Figure 3):  
• The resistivity contour line of 10 ohm-m is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock varies between 30 ft and 170 ft with a 
wide and deep trough in the middle of the segment.   
6. Segment 6 (Blaisdell Park, Figure 4):  
• The velocity contour line of 1800 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock increases rapidly from 110 ft at station 
180 to 150 ft near station 300. 
7. Segment 7 (Hekaha Street to McGrew Loop, Figure 4):  
• The velocity contour line of 1800 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of bedrock the decreases rapidly from 150 ft at station 
740 to 37 ft near station 1110, increases to 150 ft near station 2714, stays 
greater than 150 ft towards east till station 4380, and then decreases to 27 
ft at station 5320. 
8. Segment 8 (Aiea State Recreation Park, Figure 5):  
• The velocity contour line of 1800 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock increases from 40 ft at station 5350 to 
93 ft near station 6400. 
9. Segment 9 (Along Lehua Avenue starting from Kamehameha Highway to the 
bike path, Figure 5):  
• The velocity contour line of 1800 ft/s is interpreted as the top of the 
bedrock. 
• The depth to the top of the bedrock varies between 60 ft and 80 ft. 
CLOSURE 
Global Geophysics services will be conducted in a manner consistent with the level of 
care and skill ordinarily exercised by other members of the geophysical community 
currently practicing under similar conditions subject to the time limits and financial and 
physical constraints applicable to the services. However, MASW is a remote sensing 
geophysical method that may not detect all subsurface conditions, such as velocity and 
thickness variations. Furthermore, the modeled bedrock velocity may well below its true 
velocity due to the limitation of the method.  In general, the errors in the interpreted 
depths, dependent on the resolution of the technique, are estimated to be approximately 
±15 % of the true depths. 
Mr. John Chen

A P P E N D I X   D 
In-Situ Permeability Tests 
In-situ permeability tests using PQ Wire Line Packer System Type II were 
performed in seven (8) borings, as summarized below. Packer tests provide in-situ 
transmissivity (permeability) of formations with various degrees of fracture and weathering 
conditions.  Twenty-four (24) packer injection tests were conducted at selected coral and 
basalt rock intervals encountered in the borings to characterize the hydraulic properties of 
coral and basalt rocks. 
The packer testing was performed after completion of drilling and upon backfilling 
with bentonite to the selected testing interval. Two inflatable rubber packers 
(double-packer) were lowered to the testing interval.  The testing interval then was sealed 
by the pressurized rubber packers at the top and bottom. Where the bentonite layer was 
the bottom of the testing interval, one inflatable rubber packer (single-packer) was used to 
seal at the top. 
The packer tests were conducted in general accordance with ASTM D 4630 – 
Standard Test Method for Determining Transmissivity and Storage Coefficient of 
Low-Permeability Rocks by In Situ Measurements Using the Constant Head Injection Test.  
The water inflow quantity versus time was measured until steady-state flow was reached.  
Details of the packer testing apparatus are shown on Plate D-1.  The results of the packer 
testing are present on Plates D-2 through D-25. 
Seven In-Situ Percolation tests using constant head or falling head methods were 
performed in selected locations (B-6, B-12 and B-14).  The in-situ percolation tests provide 
the coefficient of permeability within the testing interval.  For the constant head testing, the 
flow rate was recorded by timing and calculating the volume as a relatively constant flow 
rate was achieved.  For the falling head testing, the water level was measured versus time 
until reached.  The results of the percolation tests are presented on Plates D-26 and D-32.

A P P E N D I X   E 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the boreholes drilled 
for the project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A and 
summarized in Table E-1.  In addition, groundwater monitoring wells were established at 
eight boring locations, including B-1, B-3, B-5, B-6, B-7, B-10, B-11 and B-14, to monitor 
groundwater seasonal fluctuations. 
To convert to the groundwater monitoring wells, the boreholes at the above 
locations were backfilled with bentonite to the depths near the proposed tunnel invert. 
2-inch diameter Schedule 40 PVC pipe consisting of 10-foot screen and solid riser for the 
remaining were placed into the boreholes.  The screen was embedded in the filter sand 
with 12 inches of cushion below the screen and 12 inches capping above the screen.  
The remaining riser was surrounded by bentonite backfill until about 3 feet below the 
ground surface.  A 12-inch diameter well cover with traffic rated plate was installed for the 
access of groundwater level monitoring.  The details of typical groundwater monitoring 
well are present on Plate E-1. 
A vibrating wire piezometer, Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon, Inc., was 
installed at each monitoring well, to monitor the groundwater levels on a near-continuous 
basis using a Model LC-2 datalogger manufactured by Geokon, Inc. Catalog sheets of the 
vibrating wire piezometer and datalogger are presented in Figures E-1 and E-2 for 
illustration purposes only. 
The groundwater level data were downloaded in the field to a laptop computer on 
a regular basis and processed in our office. Typical groundwater level data obtained by 
the piezometers are shown in Tables E-2.1 and E-2.2. Graphic presentations of the 
groundwater level data collected are provided on Plates E-2 through E-9.

FIGURE E-1 
VIBRATING WIRE PIEZOMETERS 
Standard Piezometers 
Model 4500S(H), 4500AL(V) 
The Model 4500 Standard Piezometer is designed to measure fluid
pressures such as ground water elevations and pore pressures when
buried directly in embankments, fills, etc. Also for installation inside
boreholes, observation wells and standard (>19 mm diameter)
piezometer riser pipe. The Model 4500SH is designed with a heavy
duty housing for pressures that exceed 3 MPa. The Model 4500AL is
designed for low-pressure ranges. The vented version (ALV) provides
automatic 
compensation 
for 
barometric 
pressure 
changes.
Thermistors are included to measure temperatures.* 
Specifications 
4500S 
4500SH 
4500AL(V) 
Standard Ranges 
350, 700 kPa; 
1, 2, 3 MPa 
5, 7.5 MPa 
70, 170 kPa 
Over Range 
2 × rated pressure 
Resolution 
0.025% F.S. (minimum) 
Accuracy 
±0.1% F.S. 
Linearity¹ 
<0.5% F.S. (±0.1% F.S. optional) 
Temperature Range² 
−20°C to +80°C 
Length × Diameter 
133 × 19.1 
mm 
194 × 25.4 
mm 
133 × 25.4 
mm 
¹±0.1% F.S. option available in some ranges. 
²High temperature versions available on request. 
W.O. 6016-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FIGURE E-2 
SINGLE-CHANNEL DATALOGGER 
Single-Channel Dataloggers 
Model 8002-1, 8002-1A (LC-2, LC-2A) 
The Model 8002-1 (LC-2) and Model 8002-1A (LC-2A) are
stand-alone, single-channel dataloggers, designed to read both the
vibrating wire element and the integral 
thermistor 
of any
Geokon vibrating wire sensor. The LC-2 offers an internal hard wired
transducer connection and the LC-2A provides an optional 10-pin
transducer connector for rapid sensor connection. The dataloggers
are housed inside a Fiberglass Nema 4X enclosure, are very robust,
weather-proof and particularly well-suited to operation in harsh
environments. Power is supplied by two easily accessible ‘D’ cells or
by solar panel for remote long-term applications. 16,000 arrays can
be stored (8,000 arrays when used with LogWare) before the oldest
data is over-written. Strings of LC-2 dataloggers can be daisy
chained using the RS-485 interface, and versions are available with
radios and modems to provide remote readout capabilities. Complete
with Windows® based, user-friendly software. 
Specifications 
Measurement Accuracy 
0.05% F.S. (450-4000Hz) 
Measurement 
Resolution 
1 part in 20,000 
Program Memory 
24K FLASH 
Data Memory 
320K EEPROM 
Temperature Range 
−40°C to +60°C 
L × W × H 
122 × 120 × 91 mm

A P P E N D I X   F 
Rock Index Testing 
Seventeen (17) sets of rock index tests, including Indirect Brazilian Tensile 
Strength (ASTM D 3967) and Cerchar Abrasivity Index tests were performed by 
Colorado School of Mines on selected core samples obtained from our field exploration 
and prepared in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii.  Results of the tests performed are 
summarized in Table F-1.1 through F-1.3.  Details of the testing results are also 
included in this appendix (total of 34 sheets). 
Ten (10) thin section petrographic analyses were performed by Colorado School 
of Mines on selected core samples obtained from our field exploration and prepared in 
our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii.  Results of the tests performed are summarized in 
Table F-1.1 through F-1.3.  Details of the testing results are also included in this 
appendix (total of 21 sheets).

@92'
@92'
Project: Honolulu/Waipahu/Pearl City Wastewater Facilities Plant, W.O. 6016-10  
Client:  Geolabs 
Studied by:  Sandin E. Phillipson Date:  March 20, 2010 
The samples in this suite include limestone and basalt, and appear to reflect the change in rock type and 
texture with depth from the surface into the interior of a basalt flow.  Samples B-1 @ 3.2, B-1 @ 4.1, and B-1 @ 
11 represent limestone, which can be classified as micrite (limey mud) and grainstone, composed of eroded 
coral fragments.  The micrite samples are estimated to have a Mohs Hardness of 3, and the grainstone, being 
dominated by aragonite, is estimated to have a higher Mohs Hardness of 3.7. 
Sample B-9 @ 3 could represent the transition from overlying sedimentary deposits of limestone to 
underlying basalt flows.  The sample is characterized by angular fragments of basalt breccia that are set in a 
heavily weathered matrix, which has the consistency of soil, but retains the igneous texture of aligned 
plagioclase lathes.  The sample is interpreted as carapace breccia or peperite, at the outer shell of a basalt lava 
flow.  It is likely that the heavily weathered sample is weaker than the average Mohs Hardness value of 6.05 
would suggest. 
The remaining samples represent vesicular basalt, with average Mohs Hardness values that range 
between 6.08 and 6.2, with higher values associated with greater olivine content (H = 6.5-7).  The samples 
exhibit a progression of textures based on the abundance and size of gas vesicles, with samples B-12 @ 6.2, B-
12 @ 9.3, B-12 @ 33.3, and B-12 @ 42.4 characterized by large, abundant gas vesicles that give the rock a 
frothy appearance.  The rock itself is characterized by fine-grained lathes of plagioclase that define a framework, 
which hosts fine-grained crystals of olivine in angular interstices.  These samples are interpreted to represent the 
margin of a basalt flow, where volcanic gas was able to exsolve from the lava under lower confining pressure. 
Samples B-12 @ 45.3 and B-14 @ 12.2 are characterized by a much coarser grain size and paucity of 
gas vesicles compared to the other samples of vesicular basalt.  These samples are interpreted to represent the 
interior of the basalt flow, where gas pressure was insufficient to allow exsolution due to weight and viscosity of 
the lava, and heat insulation allowed larger crystal growth compared to the other, more rapidly chilled samples.  
These samples also host coarser grains of olivine, which are prevalent in B-12 @ 45.3 and results in an average 
Mohs Hardness of 6.4, while B-14 @ 12.2 hosts abundant pyroxene and is characterized by an average Mohs 
Hardness of 6.12.

Department of Mining Engineering
Colorado School of Mines

0.2 
Calcite occurs as two grain sizes, dominantly occurring as very fine-
grained limey mud that represents the matrix of the rock; the limey 
mud matrix completely encloses and isolates irregularly shaped 
fossil fragments that are composed of fine-grained calcite crystals; 
former fossil fragments preferentially represent void spaces due to 
solution of coarser-grained calcite; other fossil fragments are 
represented in the mud matrix by dark, ghost outlines. 
Voids 
37 
- 
.03-3.6 
Irregularly shaped void spaces are preferentially localized within 
fossil fragments that have experienced dissolution of fine-grained 
calcite grains. 
    Weighted Average:  
3 
Remarks:  The texture of this rock is characterized by a matrix of limey mud that hosts scattered fossil 
fragments, which are composed of fine-grained calcite.  Recognizable fossils include gastropods and 
foraminifera.  The relatively more coarse-grained calcite has experienced preferential dissolution, such that 
many former fossil fragments represent void spaces with a ring of fine-grained calcite.  Some of the fossil 
fragments may be composed of aragonite.

at 48' depth
B-1 @ 3.2.  Angular fossil detritus is composed of calcite or aragonite (brightly speckled), isolated by the 
surrounding matrix of limey mud (brown).  Some fossil fragments have experienced dissolution, forming voids 
(purplish black).  Field of view 1 mm at 100X, taken under crossed polars.

at 85.5' depth
B-1 @ 4.1.  The rock is dominated by a matrix of limey mud (brown), which highlights the honeycomb

Thin Section Petrographic Analysis
at 107' depth
B-1 @ 11.  Angular grains of aragonite (brightly speckled) represent coral grains that are tightly packed 
together and interlock along straight and angular boundaries.  The coral matrix hosts fragments of crinoid stems 
(dusty brown) and angular void spaces (purplish black).  Field of view 1 mm at 100X, taken under crossed 
polars.

6.05 
Remarks:  The texture of this rock is characterized by basalt fragments, which may represent carapace breccia 
or peperite fragments, which are surrounded by a heavily iron-stained matrix that has been altered to the 
consistency of soil while retaining igneous textures such as aligned plagioclase lathes.  Intact, angular basalt 
fragments are characterized by fine-grained, rectangular lathes of plagioclase that exhibit flow foliation 
alignment.   The rock may be softer than suggested by the high average Mohs Hardness.

at 80.5' depth
B-9 @ 3.  Angular fragments of basalt breccia, composed of strongly aligned plagioclase lathes (white) in a 
heavily iron-stained matrix, are intermixed with a heavily weathered basalt matrix that in hand sample has the 
characteristics of soil, but in thin section retains some original igneous matrix in the form of aligned plagioclase 
lathes.  Field of view 2.4 mm at 40X, taken under crossed polars.

.02-2.5 
Thin, rectangular lathes and needles are moderately to strongly 
aligned to define a flow foliation, with the long axes of crystals 
diverted around large phenocrysts of olivine; the similarly-sized 
crystals commonly touch along tangential boundaries, with the 
remainder of lathes encased in a fine-grained matrix that hosts very 
fine-grained crystals of plagioclase.

8 
6.5-7 
.04-1.4

4 
5-6 
.08-0.4 
Rectangular crystals are sparsely distributed throughout the rock,

    Weighted Average:  
6.1 
Remarks:  The texture of this rock is characterized by a matrix of similarly sized, rectangular lathes of 
plagioclase that are aligned to define a flow foliation.  Angular olivine and rectangular pyroxene crystals are 
sporadically distributed throughout the rock, isolated between interlocking plagioclase lathes.  The rock hosts 
sporadically distributed gas vesicles.  Although a small magnet is attracted to the hand sample, individual 
crystals of magnetite are not visible due to the dark iron staining in the rock matrix.  Based on the attraction of 
the magnet, it is estimated that the rock contains a few percent magnetite.

at 113.5' depth
B-12 @ 29.3.  Thin lathes of plagioclase (white) are generally randomly oriented, although the long axes of

Remarks:  The texture of this rock is characterized by abundantly distributed, round gas vesicles, giving the 
rock a “frothy” appearance.  The rock matrix is characterized by thin lathes of plagioclase, which are 
intergrown to define a lattice framework that hosts iddingsite-altered olivine crystals in their interstices.  The 
long axes of plagioclase lathes are strongly aligned around the margins of gas vesicles.  Although the matrix is 
heavily iron stained and obscures any magnetite crystals, the sample attracts a small magnet and is estimated to 
contain a few percent magnetite.

at 118' depth
B-12 @ 33.3.  Thin, rectangular lathes of plagioclase (white) are randomly oriented, generally isolated by a 
heavily iron-stained matrix (dark brown), which obscures disseminated magnetite crystals.  Fine-grained

aligned tangential to the vesicle wall.  Field of view 2.4 mm at 40X, taken under crossed polars.

Environmental Tests 
Ten (10) sets of environmental tests, including Volatile Organics (EPA Method 
8260), Semi-Volatile Organics (EPA Method 8270), TPH (EPA Method 8015), RCRA 8 
Metals (EPA Methods 6020/6010), and PCBs (EPA Method 8082) tests were performed 
by TetstAmerica, Inc. on selected soil samples obtained from our field exploration under 
the instructions given by AECOM’s environmental engineer.  The test results were 
directly submitted to AECOM upon the completion, and also included in this appendix 
(total of 36 sheets).

A P P E N D I X   I 
Selected Data from Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project 
(East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands Segment) 
Geolabs conducted a geotechnical engineering exploration in 2009 for the 
East Kapolei to Pearl Highlands Segment of the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit 
Corridor (HHCTC) Project. Selected boring logs and laboratory test data from the 
HHCTC that may be applicable to this project are included in this appendix for 
information and reference purposes. The selected boring locations are shown on the 
Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.13.  The subsurface conditions are presented on 
Generalized Subsurface Profiles, Plates 5.1 and 5.13.

A P P E N D I X   J 
Selected Data from Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project 
(Kamehameha Highway Segment) 
Geolabs conducted a geotechnical engineering exploration in 2009 to 2010 for 
the Kamehameha Highway Segment of the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor 
(HHCTC) Project. Selected boring logs and laboratory test data from the HHCTC that 
may be applicable to this project are included in this appendix for information and 
reference purposes. The selected boring locations are shown on the Site Plans, Plates 
3.1 through 3.13.  The subsurface conditions are presented on Generalized Subsurface 
Profiles, Plates 5.1 and 5.13.
